The Correct Approach to the Numbers of Prophets and Messengers
Discussion of differing reports on numbers of prophets/messengers and the ruling to refrain from specifying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the correct Islamic stance is to refrain from specifying a precise number for all prophets and messengers, and to believe in them generally.

Core Idea
Believe in all prophets collectively; only 25 need individual knowledge.

This lesson examines the differing narrations about the total numbers of prophets and messengers. The commentator warns against committing to any specific number because it could lead to mistakenly affirming or denying prophethood. Instead, we must accept them in general, while the detailed knowledge is reserved for only twenty-five.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrations
Read the various numbers reported for messengers and prophets. Notice the variation.
These numbers come from different hadiths.
2
Understand the correct stance
The commentator states that the correct position is to refrain from specifying a number. Why? Because it might cause error in affirming or denying prophethood for someone not in reality.
3
Connect to the Quranic verse
Allah says 'Of them are those We have related to you, and of them are those We have not.' This supports the idea that we do not know all prophets individually.
4
Apply the rule
We must believe in all prophets and messengers in general ('ala al-ijmal). This is the core takeaway.
The exception of 25 will be covered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اختلفت الروايات في عدد كلّ من الرسل والأنبياء فروي أن الرسل ثلاثمائة وثلاثة عَشَرَ، وفي رواية وأربعةَ عَشَرَ، وفي رواية: وخمسة عشر. وروي: أن الأنبياء مائة ألف وأربعة وعشرون ألفاً، وفي رواية وخمسة وعشرون ألفاً، وروي أنهم ألفُ ألف ومائتا ألف وفي رواية وأربعمائة ألف وأربعة وعشرون ألفاً. الصحيحُ فيهما: الإمساك عن حضرهم في عددٍ؛ لأنه ربما أدى إلى إثبات الرسالة أو النبوة لمن ليس كذلك في الواقع، أو إلى نفي ذلك عمن هو كذلك في الواقع، وقد قال تعالى: (مِنْهُم مَّن قَصَصْنَا عَلَيْكَ وَمِنْهُم مَن لَّمْ نَقْصُصْ عَلَيْكَ) ، فيجب التصديق بأن الله رُسُلاً وأنبياء على الإجمال إلا خمسة وعشرين، فيجب معرفتهم على التفصيل كما أشار لذلك بعضهم بقوله: حَتْمٌ عَلَى كُلِّ ذِي التَّكْلِيفِ مَعْرِفَةٌ بِأَنْبِيَاءَ عَلَى التَّفْصِيلِ قَدْ عُلِمُوا فِي تِلْكَ حُجَّتُنَا مِنْهُمْ ثَمَانِيَةً مِنْ بَعْدِ عَشْرٍ، وَيَبْقَى سَبْعَةٌ وَهُمُو إدْرِيسُ هُودٌ شُعَيْبٌ صَالِحٌ وَكَذَا ذُو الْكِفْلِ آدَمُ بِالْمُخْتَارِ قَدْ خُتِمُوا (قوله: بالْمُخْتَارِ قَدْ خُتِمُوا أي: ختموا ذكراً وخارجاً به.)
And the narrations have differed concerning the number of each of the messengers and the prophets. It is narrated that the messengers are three hundred and thirteen, and in a narration: fourteen, and in a narration: fifteen. And it is narrated that the prophets are one hundred and twenty-four thousand, and in a narration: one hundred and twenty-five thousand, and it is narrated that they are one million two hundred thousand, and in a narration: four hundred and twenty-four thousand. The correct [position] regarding both is to refrain from limiting them to a number, because perhaps it may lead to affirming prophethood or messengership for one who is not so in reality, or to denying it for one who is so in reality. And Allah has said: 'Of them are those We have related to you, and of them are those We have not related to you.' So it is obligatory to affirm that Allah has sent messengers and prophets in general, except twenty-five, for whom detailed knowledge is obligatory, as some alluded to with his saying: 'It is an obligation upon every responsible person to know the prophets in detail. Those known among them are eight after ten, and seven remain: Idris, Hud, Shu'ayb, Salih, and also Dhul-Kifl, Adam, and al-Mukhtar have concluded.' His saying: 'with al-Mukhtar have concluded' means: they are concluded in mention and in reality.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage covers the issue of the exact numbers of prophets and messengers. The commentator notes that multiple narrations exist, but the correct approach is to refrain from specifying a precise number for all, because doing so could lead to errors in affirming or denying prophethood for those not mentioned in the Quran. The general rule is to believe in all prophets and messengers collectively, except for twenty-five who must be known individually. The commentator then quotes a poem enumerating these twenty-five: the eight after ten (i.e., eighteen), plus seven more making twenty-five. The poem lists Idris, Hud, Shu'ayb, Salih, Dhul-Kifl, Adam, and al-Mukhtar (Muhammad peace be upon him). The phrase 'قَدْ خُتِمُوا' is explained to mean that these twenty-five are the ones concluded in mention and in reality; i.e., they are the ones we must know by name.

Misconception: Some think that the exact number of prophets (e.g., 124,000) is established and must be believed in. The passage corrects this by saying the correct stance is to refrain from specifying a number.
Correction: The correct stance is not to commit to any specific number due to potential error. We believe in all prophets generally without enumerating them.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter conflicting reports about religious matters, do you feel pressure to pick a side or accept uncertainty? This lesson teaches you to refrain. In what area of your life are you insisting on certainty where Allah has not given it? Will you commit to silence or saying 'Allahu a'lam' until clear proof comes?
?

How can you train your heart to be content with general knowledge (ijmal) where details are absent?

The Obligation to Know the 25 Prophets in Detail
Enumeration of the 25 prophets that must be known in detail, with a poetic mnemonic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which twenty-five prophets must be known individually and why detailed knowledge of them is obligatory.

Core Idea
Know the 25 prophets by name; all others believed in generally.

While we believe in all prophets generally, there is a subset of twenty-five prophets that every Muslim must know in detail. This lesson presents the poetic enumeration of these prophets, listing seven specifically and implying the rest. The commentator clarifies that these twenty-five are the ones we must know individually.

Guided Path
1
Read the exception
The passage states that except for twenty-five, we must know them in detail. Read the exception clause carefully.
2
Identify the seven named prophets
The poem lists seven prophets by name: Idris, Hud, Shu'ayb, Salih, Dhul-Kifl, Adam, and al-Mukhtar (Muhammad). Find these in the text.
3
Understand the 'eight after ten'
The poem mentions 'eight after ten' which together with the seven make twenty-five. The eight after ten are likely the ten plus eight? Actually it says 'from them eight after ten' meaning 18, then the remaining 7. So the total of named and unnamed known ones is 25.
The commentator does not name the 18, but they are the well-known prophets.
4
Learn the obligation
The poem says 'It is an obligation upon every responsible person to know the prophets in detail.' This reinforces the duty.

وقد اختلفت الروايات في عدد كلّ من الرسل والأنبياء فروي أن الرسل ثلاثمائة وثلاثة عَشَرَ، وفي رواية وأربعةَ عَشَرَ، وفي رواية: وخمسة عشر. وروي: أن الأنبياء مائة ألف وأربعة وعشرون ألفاً، وفي رواية وخمسة وعشرون ألفاً، وروي أنهم ألفُ ألف ومائتا ألف وفي رواية وأربعمائة ألف وأربعة وعشرون ألفاً. الصحيحُ فيهما: الإمساك عن حضرهم في عددٍ؛ لأنه ربما أدى إلى إثبات الرسالة أو النبوة لمن ليس كذلك في الواقع، أو إلى نفي ذلك عمن هو كذلك في الواقع، وقد قال تعالى: (مِنْهُم مَّن قَصَصْنَا عَلَيْكَ وَمِنْهُم مَن لَّمْ نَقْصُصْ عَلَيْكَ) ، فيجب التصديق بأن الله رُسُلاً وأنبياء على الإجمال إلا خمسة وعشرين، فيجب معرفتهم على التفصيل كما أشار لذلك بعضهم بقوله: حَتْمٌ عَلَى كُلِّ ذِي التَّكْلِيفِ مَعْرِفَةٌ بِأَنْبِيَاءَ عَلَى التَّفْصِيلِ قَدْ عُلِمُوا فِي تِلْكَ حُجَّتُنَا مِنْهُمْ ثَمَانِيَةً مِنْ بَعْدِ عَشْرٍ، وَيَبْقَى سَبْعَةٌ وَهُمُو إدْرِيسُ هُودٌ شُعَيْبٌ صَالِحٌ وَكَذَا ذُو الْكِفْلِ آدَمُ بِالْمُخْتَارِ قَدْ خُتِمُوا (قوله: بالْمُخْتَارِ قَدْ خُتِمُوا أي: ختموا ذكراً وخارجاً به.)
And the narrations have differed concerning the number of each of the messengers and the prophets. It is narrated that the messengers are three hundred and thirteen, and in a narration: fourteen, and in a narration: fifteen. And it is narrated that the prophets are one hundred and twenty-four thousand, and in a narration: one hundred and twenty-five thousand, and it is narrated that they are one million two hundred thousand, and in a narration: four hundred and twenty-four thousand. The correct [position] regarding both is to refrain from limiting them to a number, because perhaps it may lead to affirming prophethood or messengership for one who is not so in reality, or to denying it for one who is so in reality. And Allah has said: 'Of them are those We have related to you, and of them are those We have not related to you.' So it is obligatory to affirm that Allah has sent messengers and prophets in general, except twenty-five, for whom detailed knowledge is obligatory, as some alluded to with his saying: 'It is an obligation upon every responsible person to know the prophets in detail. Those known among them are eight after ten, and seven remain: Idris, Hud, Shu'ayb, Salih, and also Dhul-Kifl, Adam, and al-Mukhtar have concluded.' His saying: 'with al-Mukhtar have concluded' means: they are concluded in mention and in reality.

Commentary
This passage covers the issue of the exact numbers of prophets and messengers. The commentator notes that multiple narrations exist, but the correct approach is to refrain from specifying a precise number for all, because doing so could lead to errors in affirming or denying prophethood for those not mentioned in the Quran. The general rule is to believe in all prophets and messengers collectively, except for twenty-five who must be known individually. The commentator then quotes a poem enumerating these twenty-five: the eight after ten (i.e., eighteen), plus seven more making twenty-five. The poem lists Idris, Hud, Shu'ayb, Salih, Dhul-Kifl, Adam, and al-Mukhtar (Muhammad peace be upon him). The phrase 'قَدْ خُتِمُوا' is explained to mean that these twenty-five are the ones concluded in mention and in reality; i.e., they are the ones we must know by name.

The Meaning of Wujub in Prophetic Attributes
Clarifying the meaning of wujub and its types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific meaning of wujub in this theological context and how it encompasses both legal and rational necessity.

Core Idea
Wujub here means non-contrariety, even if established by a legal proof.

The commentator begins by clarifying the term wujub as it applies to the attributes of prophets. This is not simply legal obligation but a broader necessity that can be established through different types of evidence. Understanding this distinction is key to the subsequent debate about the indication of miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening definition
Read the first two lines of the passage where the commentator defines wujub as 'عدم الانفكاك' and then explains the two types for different attributes.
2
Distinguish the two types
Notice that the wujub of amanah and tabligh is by legal proof, while the wujub of sidq is by rational or conventional proof depending on the view.
This distinction is important for the later debate.
3
Reflect on the scope
Consider why the commentator says 'wujub here is more general than legal and rational'. This sets the stage for the discussion of miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : فَيَجِبُ فِي حَقِّهِمْ... إلخ) المراد بالوجوب هنا: عدم الانفكاك ولو بالدليل الشرعي؛ لأن وجوب الأمانة والتبليغ بدليل شرعي، وأما وجوب الصدق؛ فبدليل عقلي بناءً على أن دلالة المعجزة عقلية، أو وضعي بناءً على أن دلالتها وضعيةً؛ لأنها مُنَزَّلَةٌ منزلة قوله تعالى: صَدَقَ عبدي... إلخ، ودلالته وضعية، وهذا هو ظاهرُ كلام المصنف فيما يأتي. والصحيح أنه عادي بناءً على أن دلالتها عادية أي: مُسْتَدَةٌ للعادة الجارية بأن تلك المعجزة علامة على الصدق. (قوله: الصدْقُ) أي: مطابَقَةُ الخبر للواقع. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري. (قوله: بِدَلِيلِ شَرْعِيّ لأن الملازمة فيهما بين المقدم والتالي لم تثبت إلا بالدليل الشرعي المشار إليه بقوله الآتي: لأن الله أمرنا بالاقتداء بهم... إلخ). (قوله: أوْ وَضْعِيَّةٌ) في بعض النسخ أو وضعي وهو متعين بدليل قوله : (بناء... إلخ) تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي. (قوله: الْمُرَادُ بِالْوُجُوبِ... إلخ) أي: الوجوب هنا أعم من الوجوب الشرعي والعقلي؛ لأن وجوب الأمانة والتبليغ شرعيّ؛ لثبوت ذلك الوجوب بالدليل الشرعي على المعتمد، ووجوب الصدق عقلي بناءً على أن دلالة المعجزة عقلية اهـ دُسُوقي وشرقاوي. وتوقف فيه بعضهم. تأمل. (قوله: بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّ دِلَالَةَ الْمُعْجِزَةِ عَقْلِيَّةٌ :أي: إنها تدل عقلاً على صِدْقِ الآتي بها؛ لأن الله تعالى ما أَوْجَدَ ذلك الخارقَ على يد الرسول إلا مُريداً تصديقه به. ورُدَّ: بأن ذلك ليس بلازم عقلاً؛ لأن إيجاد الله ذلك الخارق لا يدلُّ عقلاً على كونه أراد به تصديق الرسول، وإنما يدلُّ عقلاً على كونه تعالى أراد وقوع ذلك الخارق مجرَّداً عن إرادة التصديق وعدمها. (قوله: وَالصَّحِيحُ : أَنَّهُ عَادِيٌّ ولا يقال: الأمرُ العادي يصح تخلفه، فلا تدل المعجزة حينئذٍ على صدق الرسول قطعاً؛ لأنا نقول: القطعُ يُجَامِعُ الأمر العادي، ألا ترى أنك تكذب بمقتضى العادة من يقول: الجبل الفلاني ذَهَب مع إمكان تخلفه عقلاً؛ إذ لو فرضنا أن الله خَلَقَة ذهباً لم يلزم عليه محال .اهـ. دسوقي
His saying: 'So it is necessary in their regard... etc.' The intended meaning of wujub here is: non-contrariety (i.e., necessary existence) even if by a legal proof (dalil shar'i); because the necessity of trustworthiness (amanah) and conveying (tabligh) is by a legal proof, while as for the necessity of truthfulness (sidq), it is by a rational proof (dalil 'aqli) based on the view that the indication of the miracle is rational, or by a conventional proof (wad'i) based on the view that its indication is conventional, because it is treated like the saying of Allah: 'My servant has spoken truthfully... etc.', and its indication is conventional, and this is the apparent meaning of the author's words in what follows. And the correct view is that it is habitual ('adi) based on the view that its indication is habitual, i.e., derived from the prevalent habit that the miracle is a sign of truthfulness. (His saying: 'al-sidq') means: correspondence of the report with reality. Gloss by al-'Allamah Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'by a legal proof' because the implication between the antecedent and consequent in them is only established by the legal proof indicated by his subsequent saying: 'Because Allah commanded us to follow them... etc.'). (His saying: 'or conventional') in some copies 'or conventional' and it is determinative due to his saying: 'based on... etc.' from the notes of al-Allamah Muhammad al-Anbabi. (His saying: 'The intended meaning of wujub... etc.') means: wujub here is more general than legal (shar'i) and rational ('aqli), because the necessity of amanah and tabligh is legal, due to the establishment of that obligation by legal proof according to the relied-upon view, and the necessity of sidq is rational based on the view that the indication of the miracle is rational – end of Dusuki and Sharqawi. And some hesitated about it. Reflect. (His saying: 'Based on the view that the indication of the miracle is rational') means: it indicates rationally the truthfulness of the one who brings it, because Allah only created that extraordinary event at the hand of the messenger intending to affirm him. And it was refuted: that is not a rational necessity, because Allah's creation of that extraordinary event does not rationally indicate that He intended to affirm the messenger by it; rather it rationally indicates that He intended the occurrence of that extraordinary event free from the intention of affirmation or its absence. (His saying: 'And the correct view is that it is habitual') and one does not say: a habitual matter may be subject to exception, so the miracle does not then indicate the truthfulness of the messenger with certainty; for we say: certainty is compatible with habitual matters. Do you not see that you deny by virtue of habit the one who says: 'such-and-such mountain has turned to gold' despite the possibility of its happening rationally? For if we assume that Allah created it as gold, nothing impossible would entail. End of Dusuki.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the attributes that are wajib (necessary) for prophets, specifically sidq (truthfulness), amanah (trustworthiness), and tabligh (conveying the message). The commentator first clarifies the meaning of 'wujub' here: it is not limited to legal obligation (shar'i) but can also be rational ('aqli) or conventional (wad'i). He distinguishes between amanah and tabligh, whose necessity is established by legal proof, and sidq, whose necessity is debated: some say it is rational based on the rational indication of miracles, others say it is conventional because the miracle is treated like Allah's statement 'My servant has spoken truthfully'. The correct view, according to the commentator, is that the indication of the miracle is habitual ('adi), meaning that habit (custom) has established that a miracle signifies the truthfulness of the claimant. He then defines sidq as the correspondence of a report with reality. The subsequent glosses by Ahmad al-Ajhuri, Muhammad al-Anbabi, Dusuki, and Sharqawi further elaborate: the wujub of amanah and tabligh is legal (shar'i) because it is based on the command to follow the prophets, while the wujub of sidq is rational according to the rational indication view. The rational view is presented: because Allah only creates miracles to affirm the messenger, it rationally implies truthfulness. However, this is refuted: one cannot rationally infer that Allah intended affirmation from the mere occurrence of the miracle; the miracle alone does not necessitate any specific intention. The correct view is that it is habitual, and the objection that habitual matters are not certain is answered: certainty can coexist with habitual occurrences; for example, we deny a claim that a mountain turned to gold based on habit, even though it is rationally possible. Thus, the miracle gives certainty about the prophet's truthfulness based on the habitual connection established by Allah.

Key Terms
وجوب
أمانة
تبليغ
صدق
دليل شرعي
دليل عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All wajib attributes of prophets are proven by the same type of evidence.
Correction: The passage shows that amanah and tabligh are proven by legal proof, while sidq is proven by rational or conventional proof, depending on the view.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'wujub' mean in this context?
A) Legal obligation
B) Non-contrariety, even by legal proof
C) Rational necessity
D) Conventional necessity

Key Takeaway
Wujub for prophets means the attribute must be present, but the type of evidence for its necessity differs: legal for amanah/tabligh and rational/conventional for sidq.

The Three Views on the Indication of Miracles
Enumeration of the three views on miraculous indication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three opinions on how a miracle indicates the prophet's truthfulness: rational, conventional, and habitual.

Core Idea
Miracles indicate truthfulness by habit, not by rational necessity.

This passage presents a key theological debate: what kind of evidence does a miracle provide for the truthfulness of a prophet? Three views are discussed, and the correct one is that the indication is habitual ('adi). This lesson will guide you through each view and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three views
Read the part where the views are listed: rational, conventional, and habitual. Notice the basis for each.
2
Understand the rational and conventional views
The rational view holds that the miracle rationally implies truthfulness; the conventional view treats the miracle as Allah's statement.
The passage mentions that the conventional view is the apparent meaning of the author.
3
Recognize the correct view
The commentator states 'والصحيح أنه عادي' (the correct view is that it is habitual). Understand why this is considered correct.

Source Text

(قوله: وَالصَّحِيحُ : أَنَّهُ عَادِيٌّ ولا يقال: الأمرُ العادي يصح تخلفه، فلا تدل المعجزة حينئذٍ على صدق الرسول قطعاً؛ لأنا نقول: القطعُ يُجَامِعُ الأمر العادي، ألا ترى أنك تكذب بمقتضى العادة من يقول: الجبل الفلاني ذَهَب مع إمكان تخلفه عقلاً؛ إذ لو فرضنا أن الله خَلَقَة ذهباً لم يلزم عليه محال .اهـ. دسوقي

(His saying: 'The intended meaning of wujub... etc.') means: wujub here is more general than legal (shar'i) and rational ('aqli), because the necessity of amanah and tabligh is legal, due to the establishment of that obligation by legal proof according to the relied-upon view, and the necessity of sidq is rational based on the view that the indication of the miracle is rational – end of Dusuki and Sharqawi. And some hesitated about it. Reflect. (His saying: 'Based on the view that the indication of the miracle is rational') means: it indicates rationally the truthfulness of the one who brings it, because Allah only created that extraordinary event at the hand of the messenger intending to affirm him. And it was refuted: that is not a rational necessity, because Allah's creation of that extraordinary event does not rationally indicate that He intended to affirm the messenger by it; rather it rationally indicates that He intended the occurrence of that extraordinary event free from the intention of affirmation or its absence. (His saying: 'And the correct view is that it is habitual') and one does not say: a habitual matter may be subject to exception, so the miracle does not then indicate the truthfulness of the messenger with certainty; for we say: certainty is compatible with habitual matters. Do you not see that you deny by virtue of habit the one who says: 'such-and-such mountain has turned to gold' despite the possibility of its happening rationally? For if we assume that Allah created it as gold, nothing impossible would entail. End of Dusuki.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the attributes that are wajib (necessary) for prophets, specifically sidq (truthfulness), amanah (trustworthiness), and tabligh (conveying the message). The commentator first clarifies the meaning of 'wujub' here: it is not limited to legal obligation (shar'i) but can also be rational ('aqli) or conventional (wad'i). He distinguishes between amanah and tabligh, whose necessity is established by legal proof, and sidq, whose necessity is debated: some say it is rational based on the rational indication of miracles, others say it is conventional because the miracle is treated like Allah's statement 'My servant has spoken truthfully'. The correct view, according to the commentator, is that the indication of the miracle is habitual ('adi), meaning that habit (custom) has established that a miracle signifies the truthfulness of the claimant. He then defines sidq as the correspondence of a report with reality. The subsequent glosses by Ahmad al-Ajhuri, Muhammad al-Anbabi, Dusuki, and Sharqawi further elaborate: the wujub of amanah and tabligh is legal (shar'i) because it is based on the command to follow the prophets, while the wujub of sidq is rational according to the rational indication view. The rational view is presented: because Allah only creates miracles to affirm the messenger, it rationally implies truthfulness. However, this is refuted: one cannot rationally infer that Allah intended affirmation from the mere occurrence of the miracle; the miracle alone does not necessitate any specific intention. The correct view is that it is habitual, and the objection that habitual matters are not certain is answered: certainty can coexist with habitual occurrences; for example, we deny a claim that a mountain turned to gold based on habit, even though it is rationally possible. Thus, the miracle gives certainty about the prophet's truthfulness based on the habitual connection established by Allah.

عقلي
وضعي
عادي
معجزة
صدق

Misconception: Miracles prove prophethood by rational necessity.
Correction: The correct view is that the indication is habitual, not rational.

Checkpoint

A) It is rational

C) It is habitual
D) It is legal

Key Takeaway
The miracle indicates the prophet's truthfulness through habitual custom, not rational or conventional necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted the truth of Islam because you expected a more 'rational' proof, forgetting that Allah has made miracles signs by His custom? What does this say about your trust in Allah's way of guiding people?
?
If the indication of miracles is merely habitual, how does that affect your certainty about the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him)? Are you willing to accept that your certainty rests on a habit of Allah, not on your own reasoning?
?
The passage defines truthfulness (sidq) as correspondence of a report with reality. In your daily life, do you live as though your own words correspond to reality, especially when testifying about faith? Reflect on a time you exaggerated or misrepresented Islam to make it more 'reasonable'—will you repent and align your speech with truth?

Refuting the Rational View and Defending the Habitual View
Debate on the rational and habitual indication of miracles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the rational indication view and the defense of the habitual view against the objection that habitual matters are uncertain.

Core Idea
Certainty can coexist with habitual occurrences; miracles give certainty through habitual connection.

The passage does not only present views but also engages in a debate. The rational view is refuted, and an objection against the habitual view is answered. This lesson walks through the argument and counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of the rational view
The passage presents the rational view and then refutes it: the miracle does not rationally imply truthfulness because Allah's intention is not rationally deducible.
2
Understand the objection to the habitual view
Critics say that habitual matters can be exceptional, so the miracle may not be a certain sign. Read the objection.
3
Follow the response
The response uses an analogy: we deny an extraordinary claim based on habit, but that denial is certain even though it is habitual. So certainty is compatible with habit.

Meaning of Wujub and Types of Prophetic Obligations
Al-Bajuri defines wujub and distinguishes two types of prophetic obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'wujub' in this context means non-detachment and can be based on revealed or rational proof, and that the obligations of trustworthiness and delivery are revealed while truthfulness follows a different proof.

Core Idea
Wujub in Prophethood is not always logical; it can be revealed or rational.

Al-Bajuri begins by clarifying the meaning of 'wujub' (necessity) when applied to the prophets. He explains that it does not mean logical impossibility of non-existence, but rather that the attribute is inseparable from the prophet, even if the proof comes from revelation. He then distinguishes between different types of prophetic obligations and their respective proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of wujub
Read the first line: 'المراد بالوجوب هنا: عدم الانفكاك ولو بالدليل الشرعي'. Understand that wujub here means non-detachment, even if the proof is from revelation.
2
Identify the two types
Notice that trustworthiness (الأمانة) and delivery (التبليغ) are obligated by revealed proof, while truthfulness (الصدق) is obligated by either rational or conventional proof depending on the view of miracle indication.
3
Connect to miracle indication
Note that the proof for truthfulness depends on whether the indication of miracles is considered rational or conventional. This sets up the discussion in the next lesson.
This shows how foundational issues affect conclusions.

Commentary
This passage explains the meaning of 'necessity' (الوجوب) in the context of the attributes of prophets. It distinguishes three types: rational, revealed, and conventional. The main argument concerns the indication of miracles (دلالة المعجزة). Three views are presented: rational (عقلي), conventional (وضعي), and habitual (عادي). The correct view according to the author is habitual, meaning that miracles are signs of truthfulness by customary practice, not by logical necessity or convention. The passage defends this view against the objection that habitual matters can be violated, thus undermining certainty. It responds that certainty can coexist with habitual occurrence, as shown by the example of a mountain turning to gold—rationally possible but habitually impossible, yet we are certain it did not happen.

Three Views on the Indication of Miracles
Al-Bajuri presents three views on miracle indication and defends the habitual view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three views on how miracles indicate the truthfulness of a prophet (rational, conventional, habitual), and why the habitual view is considered correct according to the author.

Core Idea
Miracles indicate truthfulness by customary practice, not by logical necessity.

Al-Bajuri now turns to a key dispute: what is the precise nature of the indication of a miracle? Does it prove the prophet's truthfulness by rational necessity, by convention (as if God's direct statement), or by habit? He presents the correct view as habitual and defends it against objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the three views
Read the passage identifying the rational (عقلي), conventional (وضعي), and habitual (عادي) views. Note the description of each.
The conventional view treats the miracle like a direct statement from God: 'My servant has spoken truthfully'.
2
Understand the correct view
The author states that the correct view is habitual. Understand why: based on the customary practice that miracles are signs of truthfulness, not because of logical necessity or convention.
3
Examine the objection and response
Read the objection: habitual matters can be violated, so miracles would not produce certainty. Then read the response: certainty can coexist with the habitual, as in the example of a mountain turning to gold.
This shows that the Ash'ari position does not require logical impossibility for certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله : فَيَجِبُ فِي حَقِّهِمْ... إلخ) المراد بالوجوب هنا: عدم الانفكاك ولو بالدليل الشرعي؛ لأن وجوب الأمانة والتبليغ بدليل شرعي، وأما وجوب الصدق؛ فبدليل عقلي بناءً على أن دلالة المعجزة عقلية، أو وضعي بناءً على أن دلالتها وضعيةً؛ لأنها مُنَزَّلَةٌ منزلة قوله تعالى: صَدَقَ عبدي... إلخ، ودلالته وضعية، وهذا هو ظاهرُ كلام المصنف فيما يأتي. والصحيح أنه عادي بناءً على أن دلالتها عادية أي: مُسْتَدَةٌ للعادة الجارية بأن تلك المعجزة علامة على الصدق. (قوله: الصدْقُ) أي: مطابَقَةُ الخبر للواقع. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري. (قوله: بِدَلِيلِ شَرْعِيّ لأن الملازمة فيهما بين المقدم والتالي لم تثبت إلا بالدليل الشرعي المشار إليه بقوله الآتي: لأن الله أمرنا بالاقتداء بهم... إلخ). (قوله: أوْ وَضْعِيَّةٌ) في بعض النسخ أو وضعي وهو متعين بدليل قوله : (بناء... إلخ) تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي. (قوله: بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّ دِلَالَةَ الْمُعْجِزَةِ عَقْلِيَّةٌ :أي: إنها تدل عقلاً على صِدْقِ الآتي بها؛ لأن الله تعالى ما أَوْجَدَ ذلك الخارقَ على يد الرسول إلا مُريداً تصديقه به. ورُدَّ: بأن ذلك ليس بلازم عقلاً؛ لأن إيجاد الله ذلك الخارق لا يدلُّ عقلاً على كونه أراد به تصديق الرسول، وإنما يدلُّ عقلاً على كونه تعالى أراد وقوع ذلك الخارق مجرَّداً عن إرادة التصديق وعدمها. (قوله: وَالصَّحِيحُ : أَنَّهُ عَادِيٌّ ولا يقال: الأمرُ العادي يصح تخلفه، فلا تدل المعجزة حينئذٍ على صدق الرسول قطعاً؛ لأنا نقول: القطعُ يُجَامِعُ الأمر العادي، ألا ترى أنك تكذب بمقتضى العادة من يقول: الجبل الفلاني ذَهَب مع إمكان تخلفه عقلاً؛ إذ لو فرضنا أن الله خَلَقَة ذهباً لم يلزم عليه محال .اهـ. دسوقي
(His saying: 'So it is necessary in their right... etc.') The intended meaning of 'necessity' here is non-detachment (عدم الانفكاك) even if by a revealed proof; because the obligation of trustworthiness (الأمانة) and delivery (التبليغ) is by a revealed proof, while the obligation of truthfulness (الصدق) is by a rational proof, based on the indication of the miracle being rational, or a conventional proof, based on its indication being conventional, because it is treated as occupying the station of His saying: 'My servant has spoken truthfully... etc.', and its indication is conventional. This is the apparent meaning of the author's words later. And the correct view is that it is habitual (عادي), based on the fact that its indication is habitual, i.e., relying on the customary practice that that miracle is a sign of truthfulness. (His saying: 'Truthfulness (الصدق)') i.e., correspondence of the report to reality. The gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri. (His saying: 'by a revealed proof, because the entailment between the antecedent and consequent in both of them was only established by the revealed proof referred to by his subsequent saying: "because God commanded us to follow them... etc."') (His saying: 'or conventional') in some copies 'or conventional' and it is determined by the evidence of his saying (based... etc.) The reports of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi. (His saying: 'The intended meaning of necessity... etc.') i.e., necessity here is more general than revealed and rational necessity; because the obligation of trustworthiness and delivery is revealed, due to the establishment of that obligation by the revealed proof according to the relied-upon view, and the obligation of truthfulness is rational based on the indication of the miracle being rational. End of al-Dusuqi and al-Sharqawi. And some paused regarding it. Contemplate. (His saying: 'based on the fact that the indication of the miracle is rational' i.e., it indicates rationally the truthfulness of the one who brings it, because God Almighty did not create that supernatural event at the hand of the messenger except intending to affirm him by it. And it was refuted: that it is not rationally necessary, because God's creating that supernatural event does not indicate rationally that He intended thereby to affirm the messenger, but rather indicates rationally that He intended the occurrence of that supernatural event devoid of the intention of affirmation or its absence. (His saying: 'And the correct view is that it is habitual' and it is not said: the habitual matter may be violated, so the miracle would not then indicate the truthfulness of the messenger definitively; because we say: certainty coexists with the habitual matter. Do you not see that you deem false, by customary practice, the one who says: 'such and such mountain turned to gold' despite the possibility of its violation rationally, for if we assumed that God created it as gold, no impossibility would follow. End of al-Dusuqi.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage explains the meaning of 'necessity' (الوجوب) in the context of the attributes of prophets. It distinguishes three types: rational, revealed, and conventional. The main argument concerns the indication of miracles (دلالة المعجزة). Three views are presented: rational (عقلي), conventional (وضعي), and habitual (عادي). The correct view according to the author is habitual, meaning that miracles are signs of truthfulness by customary practice, not by logical necessity or convention. The passage defends this view against the objection that habitual matters can be violated, thus undermining certainty. It responds that certainty can coexist with habitual occurrence, as shown by the example of a mountain turning to gold—rationally possible but habitually impossible, yet we are certain it did not happen.

Key Terms
دلالة المعجزة
العادي
العقلي
الوضعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that miracles logically necessitate truthfulness (rational view) or that the indication is purely conventional like language.
Correction: The correct view is that the indication is habitual; it does not rely on logical necessity but on the customary practice established by God.

Checkpoint
According to the correct view, what type of indication does a miracle have?
A) Rational
B) Conventional
C) Habitual
D) Logical

Key Takeaway
Miracles indicate the truthfulness of prophets through habitual practice, not logical necessity, and this still provides certainty because certainties can be based on custom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about a miracle or a sign from God, do you immediately believe or do you require logical proof? How does this lesson challenge your reliance on intellectual certainty over customary signs? What would it mean for your faith if you only accepted matters that are logically necessary?
?
The lesson argues that certainty can be based on custom. Are there areas of your life where you trust custom over logic? How does that apply to your belief in prophethood? Are you willing to accept that God's customary practice is enough for conviction?
?
If miracles only indicate truth by custom, does that make you uneasy? Why? What does this teach you about the role of God's will in your conviction? Can you live with a faith that is not grounded in logical necessity but in trust in God's consistent pattern?

Definition and Three Divisions of Truthfulness
Al-Bajuri defines sidq and enumerates its three types, specifying the intended two

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three divisions of truthfulness as defined by Al-Bajuri and why only the first two are relevant for proving prophethood.

Core Idea
Truthfulness has three types, but only two are needed to prove prophethood.

Al-Bajuri begins by clarifying what truthfulness (sidq) means in the context of prophethood. He divides it into three categories to avoid confusion. The passage then explains that the proof of prophethood through miracles only establishes truthfulness in the claim of prophethood and in conveying divine rulings, not in everyday speech. This distinction is crucial for understanding the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Notice how Al-Bajuri introduces the three divisions with 'واعلم أن الصدق ثلاثة أقسام'.
The passage is from a gloss on al-Sanusiyyah.
2
Identify the three divisions
List the three divisions: truthfulness in the claim of prophethood, in conveying divine rulings, and in worldly speech. Note the examples given for the third.
3
Understand the specification
Al-Bajuri says 'والمراد هنا: القسمان الأولان' – only the first two are intended here. Understand why: the proof (miracle) only indicates these two.
The third division is included in trustworthiness (الأمانة).
4
Consider the implication
Reflect on why this distinction matters: the miracle confirms the prophet's truthfulness in his claim and his message, not every casual statement.
This avoids a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية البيجوري على السنوسية واعلم أن الصّدْقَ ثلاثة أقسام: الصدْقُ في دعوى الرسالة، والصدق في الأحكام التي يبلغونها عن الله تعالى والصدق في الكلام المتعلق بأمور الدنيا، كقام زيد، وقَعَدَ عمرو، وأكَلْتُ كذا، وشَرِبْتُ كذا، ونحو ذلك. والمراد هنا: القسمان الأولان؛ لأن البرهان الذي ذكره المصنف فيما يأتى إنما يدلُّ عليهما، وأما القسم الثالثُ؛ فهو داخل في الأمانة.
The gloss of al-Bajuri on al-Sanusiyyah: And know that truthfulness (الصدق) has three divisions: truthfulness in the claim of prophethood, truthfulness in the rulings that they convey from God Almighty, and truthfulness in speech pertaining to worldly matters, such as 'Zayd stood', 'Amr sat', 'I ate such-and-such', 'I drank such-and-such', and the like. And what is intended here are the first two sections, because the proof that the author mentioned later only indicates them. As for the third section, it is included in trustworthiness (الأمانة).
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage defines truthfulness (الصدق) and classifies it into three types: (1) truthfulness in the claim of prophethood, (2) truthfulness in conveying divine rulings, and (3) truthfulness in everyday speech. It then clarifies that in the context of proving prophethood through miracles, only the first two types are intended, because the miracle directly confirms the prophet's claim and the truth of his message. The third type (daily speech) falls under trustworthiness (الأمانة), not under the specific proof of miracles.

Key Terms

دعوى الرسالة
الأحكام البلاغية
أمور الدنيا
الأمانة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All three types of truthfulness are proved by the same miracle.
Correction: Only the first two (claim of prophethood and conveying rulings) are directly indicated by the miracle; the third (worldly speech) is part of general trustworthiness.

Checkpoint
How many divisions of truthfulness (الصدق) are mentioned in this passage, and which are intended here?
A) Three divisions; all three are intended
B) Three divisions; only the first two are intended
C) Two divisions; both are intended
D) Four divisions; only the first is intended

Key Takeaway
Truthfulness in prophethood is specifically about the claim and the message, not everyday speech, which falls under trustworthiness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The third type of truthfulness—in worldly speech—is placed under trustworthiness. In your own life, is your truthfulness in everyday speech as strong as your truthfulness in matters of faith? Which area needs more work, and what specific step will you take today to align them?

Objection on Separating Sidq from Amana
Al-Bajuri presents an objection and answer about separating sidq from amana.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the scholars explicitly separate the types of truthfulness from trustworthiness in prophetic attributes, despite their overlap.

Core Idea
In theology, the gravity of ignorance demands explicit definitions, not mere generalities.

In this passage, al-Bajuri addresses a potential redundancy objection: if truthfulness is included in trustworthiness, why mention it separately? The answer reveals a key methodological principle in kalam—the danger of ignorance requires clarity. We'll explore the objection, the response, and a further nuance about the scope of truthfulness.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the first part of the Arabic passage: the objection that the first two types of truthfulness are already inside trustworthiness, and the answer that the danger of ignorance prevents relying on generalization.
Notice the key term 'خطر الجهل' (danger of ignorance).
2
Understand the definition of trustworthiness
Read the definition of amana provided by the commentator: not committing prohibited or disliked acts, and the preservation by Allah.
This definition shows the scope of amana.
3
Consider the additional scope of truthfulness
Read the note from some teachers about the generality of truthfulness being proven by miracles, including truthfulness in non-communicative matters.
This expands the understanding of what the miracle proves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: كل من القسمين الأولين داخل أيضاً في الأمانة، بل التبليغ أيضاً داخل فيها، فلا وَجْهَ لإفراد ذلك عنها. أجيب بأنه قد تقدم أن خَطَرَ الجهل في هذا الفنّ عظيم، فلا يُكتفى فيه بالإجمال. (قوله: وَالْأَمَانَةُ) أي: عدمُ خيانتهم بفعل مُحَرَّمٍ أو مكروه وفسَّرَها بعضهم: باتصافهم بحفظ الله ظواهرهم وبواطنهم من التلبس بمنهي عنه نهي تحريم أو كراهة. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي. (قوله: وَالْمُرَادُ هُنَا الْقِسْمَانِ الْأَوْلَانِ... إلخ) قال بعض مشايخنا الذي أراه عموم الصدق في "المُنْقِذَةِ" وشرحها، فيجب في حقهم بدلالة المعجزة: الصدق، فلا يجوز عليهم الكَذِبُ؛ إذ كل من شاهَدَ المعجزة أو بَلَغَتْهُ بالتواتر؛ عَلِمَ علماً لا يَطْرُقُ الشك ساحته بأن من ظَهَرَتْ على يديه صادق في دعواه لا محالة ومن جملتها أنه لا يَكْذِبُ في غيرها اهـ. فأنت تراه زاد: (ومن جملتها... إلخ) ليشمل الصدقُ صِدْقَهم في غير الأمور البلاغية، وتكون المعجزة دالة عليه أيضاً.
For it is said: Each of the first two sections is also included in trustworthiness, indeed delivery (التبليغ) is also included in it, so there is no justification for separating them from it. It is answered: Because it was previously mentioned that the gravity [literally: danger] of ignorance in this art is great, so generalization is not sufficient in it. (His saying: 'And trustworthiness (الأمانة)') i.e., their not betraying by committing a prohibited or disliked act. And some explained it as: their being characterized by God's preservation of their outward and inward from being adorned with what is forbidden or disliked, prohibition of prohibition or dislike. The reports of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi. (His saying: 'And what is intended here are the first two sections... etc.') Some of our teachers said: What I see is the generality of truthfulness in 'al-Munqidha' and its commentary, so what is necessary in their right by the indication of the miracle is truthfulness, and lying is not permissible for them. For everyone who witnesses the miracle or it reaches him by tawatur knows with knowledge that no doubt approaches it that the one at whose hands it appeared is truthful in his claim without exception, and from that is that he does not lie in other matters. End. So you see he added (and from that is that he does not lie in other matters) to include truthfulness: their truthfulness in matters other than communicative matters, and the miracle indicates it also.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents an objection: since the first two types of truthfulness (in claim and rulings) are already included in trustworthiness (الأمانة), why separate them? The answer is that the danger of ignorance in this science is great, so one cannot rely on generalities; explicit mention is necessary. Then it defines trustworthiness as not committing prohibited or disliked acts, and adds a note from some scholars who hold that truthfulness in its full generality (including all three types) is indicated by miracles. This means that the miracle not only proves the prophet's truth in his claim and message, but also in his everyday speech.

Key Terms
الأمانة
التبليغ
الصدق
خطر الجهل
الإجمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since truthfulness is included in trustworthiness, it is redundant to mention it separately.
Correction: The scholars separated them because the danger of ignorance requires explicit clarity, and the miracle proves truthfulness even beyond trustworthiness.

Checkpoint
Why did the scholars separate the first two types of truthfulness from trustworthiness according to the answer given?
A) Because they are not actually included in trustworthiness
B) Because the proof of miracles only applies to those two types
C) Because the danger of ignorance in this art is great, so generalities are insufficient
D) Because trustworthiness does not cover truthfulness in daily speech

Key Takeaway
The separation of truthfulness from trustworthiness is not redundant but a necessary clarity due to the severity of ignorance in theological matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How would your relationship with the Prophet ﷺ change if you truly believed that every word and action of his is attested by Allah? Will you commit to reading a hadith from him today as if it were directly confirmed by a miracle?

Distinct Scopes of Prophetic Obligations
Al-Sanusi details how each prophetic obligation adds a unique restriction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how each of the three prophetic obligations—truthfulness (sidq), trustworthiness (amana), and delivery (tabligh)—has a unique additional restriction that distinguishes it from the others.

Core Idea
Each prophetic duty adds a unique restriction not covered by the others.

Al-Sanusi's Sughra al-Sughra presents a precise logical comparison of the three obligatory attributes of prophets. While they overlap in preventing violations, each one specifically prohibits a type of failure that the others do not. This passage clarifies their distinct scopes, which is essential for understanding prophetic infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage overview
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get a general sense of the three comparisons. Notice the structure: each obligation is said to "add" (يزيد) to another.
The passage is from Sanusi's Sughra al-Sughra, a foundational matn in Ash'ari kalam.
2
Identify truthfulness's additions
Find where truthfulness (الصدق) adds to trustworthiness and to delivery. Determine what each addition prevents.
Truthfulness adds to trustworthiness by preventing forgetful lies; to delivery by preventing intentional or forgetful addition.
3
Identify trustworthiness's additions
Find where trustworthiness (الأمانة) adds to truthfulness and to delivery. Note what it prevents that the others do not.
Trustworthiness adds to truthfulness by preventing violations other than verbal lies; to delivery by preventing violations outside of delivery.
4
Identify delivery's additions
Find where delivery (التبليغ) adds to truthfulness and to trustworthiness. Understand its unique prohibition of omission.
Delivery adds to truthfulness by preventing omission of commanded content; to trustworthiness by preventing forgetful omission.
5
Reflect on the overall logic
Consider how these distinctions prevent any overlap in the conceptual definitions of the three obligations. This ensures that prophets are infallible in all aspects.
The passage ends by noting that this analysis clarifies the commentators' discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: فَإِنْ قِيلَ: كُلِّ مِنَ الْقِسْمَيْنِ... إلخ) في صغرى الصغرى" للمصنف بعد ذِكْرِ وجوب الصدق والأمانة والتبليغ ما نصه: فالواجب الأول يزيد على الأمانة بمَنْعِ الكذب سهواً، ويزيدُ على التبليغ بمنع الزيادة على ما أُمِرُوا بتبليغه عمداً أو نسياناً، وتزيد الأمانة على الصدق بمَنْع وقوع المخالفة في غير كَذِبِ اللسان وعلى التبليغ بمنع المخالفة في غير التبليغ، ويزيد التبليغ على الصدق بمَنْعِ تَرْكِ شيء مما أُمِرُوا بتبليغه عمداً أو نسياناً مع لُزُوم الصِّدْقِ فيما بلغوا من ذلك، ويزيدُ على الأمانة بمنع تزك شيء مما أمروا بتبليغه نسياناً اهـ. ووَضْحَ ذلك في شرحها، وبه تعلم ما في كلام المحشي وغيره.
(His saying: 'For it is said: Each of the two sections... etc.') in 'Sughra al-Sughra' of the author, after mentioning the obligation of truthfulness, trustworthiness, and delivery, his text is: So the first obligation (truthfulness) adds to trustworthiness by preventing forgetting a lie [？], and adds to delivery by preventing addition to what they were commanded to deliver intentionally or forgetfully. And trustworthiness adds to truthfulness by preventing the occurrence of violation in other than verbal lies, and to delivery by preventing violation in other than delivery. And delivery adds to truthfulness by preventing leaving anything of what they were commanded to deliver intentionally or forgetfully, while truthfulness is necessary in what they deliver from that. And it adds to trustworthiness by preventing forgetting something of what they were commanded to deliver. End. And that was clarified in its commentary, and by it you learn what is in the speech of the commentator (al-Mahalli) and others.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage quotes from al-Sanusi's 'Sughra al-Sughra' to explain the precise logical relationships between the three obligatory attributes of prophets: truthfulness (الصدق), trustworthiness (الأمانة), and delivery (التبليغ). It details how each one adds a specific restriction not covered by the others. For example, truthfulness adds to trustworthiness the prevention of forgetful lies, while delivery adds to truthfulness the prevention of omission. This shows that although they overlap, each has a unique scope. The passage concludes by noting that this was clarified in the commentary, and from it one can understand the nuances in the commentators' speech.

الأمانة
التبليغ
الزيادة
السهو
العمد
النسيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the three obligations are essentially the same or fully overlapping.
Correction: This passage shows each obligation has a unique additional restriction, such as truthfulness preventing forgetful lies and delivery preventing omission.

Checkpoint
According to the quote from 'Sughra al-Sughra', what does the obligation of delivery (التبليغ) add to truthfulness (الصدق)?
A) Preventing lying intentionally
B) Preventing addition to what was commanded to deliver
C) Preventing leaving anything commanded to deliver intentionally or forgetfully
D) Preventing forgetful lies

Key Takeaway
The three prophetic obligations each have a unique additional restriction that distinguishes them, ensuring comprehensive infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to be truthful, do you also guard against unintentional misinformation—like forgetful slips of the tongue? How does the prophetic standard of preventing even forgetful lies expose your own negligence? What will you change today to take greater responsibility for every word you speak?
?
The prophets were entrusted with a divine message; you too have been entrusted with the truth of Islam. In what ways have you added to the message—by speaking without knowledge—or omitted parts that are inconvenient? Name one specific addition or omission you've made this week. What step will you take to return to pure delivery?
?
If every obligation has a unique restriction, what unique duty are you consistently avoiding? The passage shows that trustworthiness covers actions beyond speech. Is there an area of your life where you are trusted but you act contrary to that trust—perhaps in family, work, or community? Confront that area and ask Allah to make you worthy of the trust He has placed in you.

How Each Prophetic Attribute Exceeds the Others
Sanusi enumerates how each prophetic attribute surpasses the others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise logical distinctions between truthfulness, trustworthiness, and conveying, and how each attribute covers a different scope of perfection.

Core Idea
Each prophetic attribute prevents a specific defect that the others do not cover.

Al-Bajuri quotes a concise passage from Sanusi's earlier work that systematically compares the three necessary attributes of prophets. This is not a repetition but a precise demonstration of how each attribute uniquely exceeds the others. Understanding these distinctions deepens our appreciation of why prophets must possess all three.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the repetitive structure: each attribute is said to 'exceed' another in a specific way.
2
Identify the three attributes
The passage compares three attributes: truthfulness (sidq), trustworthiness (amana), and conveying (tabligh). List them and note the comparisons made for each pair.
3
Analyze each comparison
For each attribute, see how it exceeds the other two. What specific defect does it prevent that the other does not? Write down each difference.
For example, truthfulness exceeds trustworthiness by preventing lying inadvertently.
4
Reflect on why this matters
Ask yourself why Sanusi went to such detail. These distinctions show that the attributes are not redundant; each covers a unique aspect of prophetic perfection.
This connects to the broader lesson on the necessity of these attributes for prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في صغرى الصغرى للمصنف بعد ذكر وجوب الصدق والأمانة والتبليغ ما نصه: فالواجب الأول يزيد على الأمانة بمنع الكذب سهواً، ويزيد على التبليغ بمنع الزيادة على ما أمروا بتبليغه عمداً أو نسياناً، وتزيد الأمانة على الصدق بمنع وقوع المخالفة في غير كذِبِ اللسان وعلى التبليغ بمنع المخالفة في غير التبليغ، ويزيد التبليغ على الصدق بمنع ترك شيء مما أمروا بتبليغه عمداً أو نسياناً مع لزوم الصدق فيما بلغوا من ذلك، ويزيد على الأمانة بمنع تزك شيء مما أمروا بتبليغه نسياناً اهـ.
In 'Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā' by the author, after mentioning the obligation of truthfulness, trustworthiness, and conveying, there is the following text: 'The first obligation (truthfulness) exceeds trustworthiness by preventing lying inadvertently, and exceeds conveying by preventing addition to what they were commanded to convey, intentionally or forgetfully. Trustworthiness exceeds truthfulness by preventing the occurrence of opposition (to the command) in other than lying of the tongue, and exceeds conveying by preventing opposition in other than conveying. Conveying exceeds truthfulness by preventing leaving anything they were commanded to convey, intentionally or forgetfully, while requiring truthfulness in what they convey of that, and exceeds trustworthiness by preventing leaving anything they were commanded to convey forgetfully.'
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a direct quotation from the author (Sanusi) from his work 'Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā', which systematically compares the three necessary attributes of prophets: truthfulness (al-ṣidq), trustworthiness (al-amāna), and conveying (al-tablīgh). Al-Bajuri includes this to show how they are distinct despite overlap. The text explains the precise excesses: Truthfulness (1st) adds over trustworthiness by preventing accidental lies (covers a possibility not covered by amāna); it adds over tablīgh by preventing intentional or forgetful addition to the message. Trustworthiness (2nd) adds over truthfulness by preventing any disobedience not involving lying; it adds over tablīgh by preventing any violation not related to conveying. Conveying (3rd) adds over truthfulness by preventing omission of any commanded message (intentional or forgetful) while still requiring truthfulness in what is conveyed; it adds over trustworthiness by preventing forgetful omission (which amāna might not cover). This precise dissection shows the careful reasoning of kalam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think these three attributes are identical or redundant.
Correction: Each attribute covers a distinct scope and prevents different defects, as shown by the precise comparisons.

Checkpoint
According to the quoted passage, how does trustworthiness (al-amāna) exceed truthfulness (al-ṣidq)?
A) It prevents lying intentionally
B) It prevents opposition to the command in matters other than lying
C) It prevents addition to the message
D) It prevents forgetting

Key Takeaway
The three prophetic attributes are distinct in scope: truthfulness prevents lying, trustworthiness prevents disobedience beyond lying, and conveying prevents omission of God's message.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the distinction between truthfulness and trustworthiness challenge your own practice? Are you careful to avoid not only lies but also any disobedience that contradicts trustworthiness? What specific step will you take this week to embody full prophetic trustworthiness?
?
Which of the three attributes do you neglect most in your daily responsibilities, and what concrete change will you make to align with that prophetic standard?
?
Conveying requires not only truth but completeness. Have you ever omitted part of a message you were entrusted to deliver, whether intentionally or through forgetfulness? What will you commit to ensuring complete fidelity in conveying God's word to others?

The Nature of Trustworthiness (Amanah) and Its Link to Infallibility
Definition of trustworthiness as a faculty preventing sin, equated with infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of trustworthiness as a deeply rooted faculty and how it relates to prophetic infallibility.

Core Idea
Trustworthiness is not just honesty; it is a divine protection from sin.

Al-Bajuri begins by defining the prophetic attribute of trustworthiness (al-amāna). This quality is more than simple reliability—it is an ingrained faculty that ensures prophets are protected from committing forbidden acts. Understanding this definition sets the stage for grasping the scope of prophetic duties.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of amāna
Read the Arabic sentence defining amāna as a 'malaka rāsikha' and its translation. Notice that it is a faculty, not an action.
The term 'malaka' implies a permanent quality.
2
Connect amāna to 'iṣma
See how al-Bajuri states that in any case, amāna reduces to 'iṣma (infallibility). Reflect on why this connection is important for prophethood.
This means a prophet's trustworthiness is a form of divine protection.
3
Consider the misconception
Many people think trustworthiness is just about keeping promises. This lesson shows it is a divinely granted prevention from sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعضهم: هي مَلَكَةٌ راسخة في النفس تَمْنَعُ صاحبها من ارتكاب المنهيات، وعلى كلّ؛ فهي تَرْجِعُ إلى العِصْمَةِ التي عبر بها بعضهم.
(قوله: وَتَبْلِيغُ مَا أُمِرُوا بِتَبْلِيغِهِ لِلْخَلْقِ) احترز بقوله: (ما أمروا... إلخ) عما أمروا بكتمانه عن الخلق، وعما خُيّروا فيه، فليس تبليغ كلّ منهما واجباً، بل يجب كِتْمَانُ ما أُمِرُوا بكتمانه، ولا يجب عليهم شيءٌ فيما خُيّروا فيه، فالأقسام ثلاثة: ما أمروا بتبليغه، وما أمروا بكتمانه وما خُيروا فيه. وإنما لم يَذْكُرِ المصنف وجوبَ كتمان ما أمروا بكتمانه؛ لأنه داخل في الأمانة كما قاله في "الأسرار الإلهية".
And some said: It (trustworthiness) is a deeply rooted faculty in the soul that prevents its possessor from committing prohibited acts. In any case, it reduces to the infallibility ('iṣma) that some have expressed. (His statement: 'and conveying what they were commanded to convey to the creation') he excludes by his statement 'what they were commanded...' what they were commanded to conceal from the creation, and what they were given a choice about. So conveying is not obligatory in either of these; rather, concealing what they were commanded to conceal is obligatory, and nothing is obligatory for them regarding what they were given a choice about. Thus the categories are three: what they were commanded to convey, what they were commanded to conceal, and what they were given a choice about. The author did not mention the obligation of concealing what they were commanded to conceal because it is included in trustworthiness, as he stated in 'al-Asrār al-Ilāhiyya'.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage defines trustworthiness (al-amāna) and clarifies the scope of the prophetic obligation to convey. Al-Bajuri first cites a definition: trustworthiness is a deeply rooted faculty (malaka rāsikha) that prevents its possessor from committing forbidden acts, and he notes that this essentially refers to infallibility ('iṣma). Then he explains the author's phrase 'conveying what they were commanded to convey to the creation' by noting that it is a restrictive qualification. There are three categories of divine command regarding communication: (1) what they are commanded to convey (obligatory to convey), (2) what they are commanded to conceal (obligatory to conceal), and (3) what they are given a choice about (neither obligatory to convey nor conceal). The author did not separately mention the obligation to conceal because it falls under trustworthiness, as explained in Sanusi's 'al-Asrār al-Ilāhiyya'.

Key Terms
أمانة
ملكة
عصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Trustworthiness is simply a moral choice to be honest and keep promises.
Correction: In prophetic theology, trustworthiness (amāna) is a God-given faculty that prevents sin, not a mere human habit.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the faculty of trustworthiness prevent?
A) Forgetting prophetic messages
B) Committing prohibited acts
C) Needing to eat
D) Speaking unnecessarily

Key Takeaway
Trustworthiness in prophets is a divinely implanted faculty that ensures they are protected from sin, essentially synonymous with infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on your own willpower to avoid sin, only to fail? How does knowing that true trustworthiness is a divinely implanted faculty, not a human effort, change how you will seek protection from sin today?
?
If the prophets were infallible because of this faculty, and you are not, what does that imply about your need for constant reliance on Allah's mercy? Will you make a sincere du'a asking Allah to grant you a share of this amāna?
?
The passage says trustworthiness 'reduces to infallibility.' When you are tempted, do you act as if your own resolve is enough, or do you recognize that only divine protection can truly guard you? What concrete step will you take this week to remind yourself of your dependence on Allah?

The Three Categories of What Prophets Are Commanded
Explanation of three categories of prophetic command: convey, conceal, choose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of divine command regarding prophetic communication: conveyance, concealment, and choice.

Core Idea
Prophets have three types of communication obligations—some must be conveyed, some concealed, and some are optional.

In this passage, al-Bajuri explains a crucial nuance: prophets are not obligated to convey every piece of information. The author's statement 'what they were commanded to convey' restricts their obligation to convey only certain matters. There are three categories: matters commanded to be conveyed, matters commanded to be concealed, and matters left to their choice. This lesson unpacks these categories and why the author omitted explicit mention of concealment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Read the Arabic passage and find the three categories: ما أمروا بتبليغه, ما أمروا بكتمانه, وما خيروا فيه. Understand what each means.
Notice the use of 'amr (command) for the first two and takhyīr (choice) for the third.
2
Understand the restriction
Al-Bajuri explains that the author's phrase 'what they were commanded to convey' excludes the other two categories. Conveying is only obligatory for the first category.
3
Reflect on why concealment is not explicitly mentioned
Al-Bajuri says the author did not mention the obligation to conceal separately because it is included in trustworthiness (amāna). Consider why trustworthiness implies concealment of certain matters.
This links back to the definition of amāna.

Source Text

(قوله: وَتَبْلِيغُ مَا أُمِرُوا بِتَبْلِيغِهِ لِلْخَلْقِ) احترز بقوله: (ما أمروا... إلخ) عما أمروا بكتمانه عن الخلق، وعما خُيّروا فيه، فليس تبليغ كلّ منهما واجباً، بل يجب كِتْمَانُ ما أُمِرُوا بكتمانه، ولا يجب عليهم شيءٌ فيما خُيّروا فيه، فالأقسام ثلاثة: ما أمروا بتبليغه، وما أمروا بكتمانه وما خُيروا فيه. وإنما لم يَذْكُرِ المصنف وجوبَ كتمان ما أمروا بكتمانه؛ لأنه داخل في الأمانة كما قاله في "الأسرار الإلهية".

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?

In your own life, do you treat matters of faith as either commanded or forbidden, leaving no room for personal discretion? Prophetic Infallibility and Impossibility of Opposite Attributes
Al-Bajuri defines the impossibility of prophetic opposite attributes and the comprehensive nature of prophetic infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the prophetic doctrine of 'ismah (infallibility) and the impossibility of all opposite attributes including sins, and how to reconcile apparent contrary evidence.

Core Idea
Prophets are sinless from all sins, even forgetful ones, before and after prophethood.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary on Umm al-Barahin clarifies an essential tenet of Sunni theology: the complete protection (isma) of prophets from all sins. Al-Bajuri defines the impossibility of opposite attributes, explains the scope of sinlessness, and addresses potential objections. Understanding this teaching is crucial for properly interpreting scriptural accounts that might imply prophetic error.

Guided Path
1
Understand the technical meaning of impossibility
Read al-Bajuri's definition of 'istiḥāla' (impossibility) in this context. Note that it means an attribute cannot be ascribed to a prophet even if a legal proof suggests otherwise.
The concept is tied to what is obligatory by legal proof: if something is wajib, its opposite is impossible by the same proof.
2
Grasp the scope of 'opposites'
Al-Bajuri clarifies that 'opposites' here means absolute contradiction, not strict logical opposites. Some opposites, like lying, are non-entities.
Lying is defined as non-correspondence to reality, making it a privation, not a real opposite.
3
Recognize the comprehensive nature of sinlessness
Focus on the statement that no sin—minor or major, forgetful or intentional, before or after prophethood—can occur from prophets. Notice how the passage refutes the objection that forgetful or pre-prophethood acts are not sins.
The response is that such acts are in the form of sin and must be interpreted.
4
Learn the principle of interpreting problematic reports
The passage concludes by stating that any evidence suggesting a prophet committed a sin must be interpreted (ta'wil). The example of Dhu al-Yadayn is mentioned as an instance.
This principle protects the doctrine of infallibility while accepting apparent scriptural challenges.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَيَسْتَحِيلُ فِي حَقِّهِمْ... إلخ) المراد بالاستحالة: عدم إمكان الاتصاف ولو بالدليل الشرعي؛ لأن ما وَجَبَ بدليل شرعي يستحيل ضده بدليل شرعي، وما جب بغيره يستحيل ضده بغيره كما تقدم تفصيله.
(قوله: أَضْدَادُ هَذِهِ الصَّفَاتِ) المراد بالضدّ هنا: مُطلَقُ المنافي؛ لأنها ليست كلَّها أضداداً كما تقدم نظيره.
(قوله: الْكَذِبُ) أي: عدم مطابَقَةِ الخبر للواقع؛ لما عُلِمَ من تعريف الصدق.
(قوله: بِفِعْلِ شَيْءٍ... إلخ) المراد بالفعل: ما يشمل القول. واعلم أنه لا فَرْقَ بين الصغيرة والكبيرة، فلا تَقَعُ منهم صغيرة ولا كبيرة ولو سهواً، قبل البعثة وبعدها.
لا يقال: ما كان سَهْواً أو قبل البعثة ليس بمعصية؛ لأنا نقول: هو صورة معصية، وما وَرَدَ مما يوهِمُ وقوع ذلك منهم يجب تأويله.
(قوله: لأَنَّهَا لَيْسَتْ كُلَّهَا أَضْدَاداً) لأن منها الكذب، وهو عدم مطابقة الخبر للواقع، فيكون أمراً عدمياً، فلا يكون ضداً حقيقة.
(قوله: وَمَا وَرَدَ... إلخ) كقوله في قصة ذي اليدين لما قال له: أقصرت الصلاة؟
(His statement: 'And it is impossible in their case... etc.') The meaning of impossibility (al-istiḥāla) is the lack of possibility of being described (with that attribute) even by a legal proof; because what is obligatory by a legal proof, its opposite is impossible by a legal proof, and what is obligatory by other than it, its opposite is impossible by other than it, as the detail was given earlier. (His statement: 'The opposites of these attributes') The meaning of opposite here is absolute contradiction; because not all of them are opposites, as was mentioned earlier in its parallel. (His statement: 'Lying (al-kadhib)') i.e., the non-correspondence of the report to reality; based on what is known from the definition of truthfulness. (His statement: 'By doing something... etc.') The meaning of 'doing' includes speech. Know that there is no distinction between a minor sin and a major sin; so neither a minor nor a major sin occurs from them, even forgetfully, before the mission and after it. It cannot be said: What was forgetful or before the mission is not a sin; for we say: It is the form of a sin, and whatever has come that implies the occurrence of that from them must be interpreted. (His statement: 'Because not all of them are opposites') Because among them is lying, which is the non-correspondence of the report to reality, so it is a non-entity, thus it is not truly an opposite. (His statement: 'And what has come... etc.') Like his statement in the story of Dhu al-Yadayn when he said to him: 'Has the prayer been shortened?'
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage explains the impossibility (istiḥāla) of the opposites of the necessary prophetic attributes. Al-Bajuri clarifies that 'impossibility' here means that it is impossible for a prophet to be described with those opposites even if the legal proof (dalīl sharʿī) would indicate obligation—because if something is obligatory by legal proof, its opposite is impossible by that same proof. The term 'opposites' (aḍdād) is used loosely to mean absolute contradiction, not strict logical opposites, since some, like lying, are non-entities (ʿadamī) and not true opposites. Lying is defined as non-correspondence of the report to reality. The prohibition of 'doing something' includes speech. A crucial point: there is no difference between minor and major sins; prophets are protected from all sins, whether done forgetfully, before or after prophethood. If someone objects that a sin committed forgetfully or before prophethood is not a sin, the answer is that it is the form (ṣūra) of a sin, and any apparent evidence suggesting otherwise must be interpreted. The passage ends with an example from the story of Dhu al-Yadayn (a hadith where the Prophet mistakenly shortened prayer), indicating that such reports require interpretation.

صغيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe prophets can commit minor sins or forgetful sins, or that sins before prophethood are not considered sins.
Correction: This passage asserts that prophets are completely protected from all sins, regardless of type, intention, or timing; any apparent contrary evidence must be interpreted.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how should apparent reports that suggest a prophet committed a sin be treated?
A) They are accepted literally
B) They must be rejected
C) They must be interpreted (ta'wil)
D) They apply only after prophethood

Key Takeaway
Prophets are completely protected from all sins, including minor, major, forgetful, and pre-prophethood sins; any apparent contrary evidence must be interpreted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage insists that any report suggesting a prophet sinned must be interpreted. Is your heart truly at peace with that, or do you find yourself doubting the prophets' absolute innocence when you read such reports? What does that reveal about your trust in the divine selection of messengers and your submission to the scholars who safeguard this belief?
?
Prophets are protected from even forgetful sins. How does that challenge your own excuses for sins you commit 'without thinking' or 'by accident'? Are you willing to take full responsibility for every sin, including those you dismissed as unintentional, knowing that even the best of creation never slipped in that way? What specific step will you take today to eliminate one habitual 'forgetful' sin?
?
The definition of lying as 'non-correspondence to reality' sets an absolute standard for truth. Can you honestly say that every report you speak corresponds exactly to reality, or do you allow yourself small distortions, exaggerations, or omissions? How would your relationships change if you adopted prophetic truthfulness as your standard, even at personal cost? Commit now to one situation where you will correct a previous untruth.
